Gemini co-leads on project origins and what's next

Google for Developers(2.6M)·42:00·7.3K views·2026-05-29
Logan KilpatrickHost & AI Advocate, Google Jeff DeanChief Scientist, Google Noam ShazeerCo-lead of Gemini, Google
TL;DRThe Gemini project was established by consolidating Google's fragmented AI research teams and compute resources into a unified effort.
This strategic alignment has accelerated multimodal "world modeling," yielded highly efficient model distillation (packing "Pro"...

TL;DR

The Gemini project was established by consolidating Google's fragmented AI research teams and compute resources into a unified effort. This strategic alignment has accelerated multimodal "world modeling," yielded highly efficient model distillation (packing "Pro" capabilities into "Flash" versions), and set the stage for agentic self-learning systems.

The Origins and Consolidation of Project Gemini

  • Eliminating Fragmentation: Prior to Gemini, Google's AI efforts were split across DeepMind and Google Brain (working on Pathways, PaLM, and PaLM 2). Jeff Dean authored a half-page memo arguing that splitting compute and talent was "silly" and that Google must consolidate into a single, unified team to build a frontier model.
  • The "Gemini" Name: The project's name stems from "twins," representing the map-and-reduce fusion of Google’s two premier AI research groups (and nod to Jeff Dean having twins).
  • Deep Collaborative Roots: The leadership team shares decades of history:
      • Jeff Dean recruited Noam Shazeer to Google in 2000 and acted as his engineering mentor.
      • Dean recruited Oriol Vinyals in 2012 to the Google Brain team to work on early deep learning models and distillation.
      • Dean and Koray Kavukcuoglu first collaborated by reviewing raw codebase directories together during Google's acquisition of DeepMind.

Model Performance, Distillation, and the "Flash" Phenomenon

  • The Generation Leap: A consistent, positive surprise for the team is that each subsequent "Flash" generation successfully packs the intelligence of the previous generation’s larger "Pro" model, accelerating efficiency.
  • The Distillation Recipe: Rather than using complex ensembles of dozens of models, Google uses a highly optimized student-teacher distillation process with modest tweaks to the original distillation paper.
  • Multimodal Joint Training: By training jointly across text, video, audio, and physical world datasets (such as robotics, lidar, and genomics), the model develops "world modeling" capabilities—understanding and simulating visual consistency, depth, and physical dynamics.
  • The Evaluation Bottleneck: Robustly evaluating LLM capabilities in a way that aligns with real-world user experiences (rather than overfitting to leaked benchmarks) remains one of the most persistent and critical challenges in frontier AI development.

Predictions and Technical Frontiers for 2027

  • Self-Learning and Agentic Research: The team predicts that within a few years, models will use agentic workflows to actively research, run experiments, and improve subsequent generations of Gemini under human guidance.
  • Dynamic Continual Learning: Researchers are aiming for a paradigm shift where models dynamically update their internal knowledge bases through interaction and experience without needing expensive weight updates.
  • Tool Latency as a Bottleneck: As autonomous agents run for longer periods (e.g., executing tasks over 30 days), the primary bottleneck will be the latency of current digital tools, which were designed for human speed rather than model speed.
  • The Efficiency Gap: Humans require vastly less data to learn. A human hears roughly one billion words in a lifetime, whereas models require trillions of tokens to achieve similar capabilities. Closing this data-efficiency gap is a key algorithmic priority.

The Evolution of Google's Product Strategy

  • The "One Box" Realization: Modern general-purpose models fulfill Google’s early search philosophy of a single "one-box" interface, but with a unified backend instead of fragmented custom engines.
  • The Ecosystem Debate: The leadership team debated whether AI will eventually consolidate into a single central "model product" or diverge into thousands of specialized products:
      • Single Model View: Oriol Vinyals noted that there is clearly one unified model powering the backend, though users may still prefer distinct digital interfaces for separation of concerns.
      • Multitude of Products: Koray Kavukcuoglu expects a massive expansion of niche products because general intelligence will make it incredibly cheap and easy to build specialized applications.

Notable Quotes

"This is silly. We are fragmenting our efforts and fragmenting our compute. And if we're going to build an incredibly powerful model, we need to all come together and work on building a single model." — Jeff Dean
"It's like squeezing the lemon. You squeeze the lemon, the juice comes out, it's the good bits, you put it in a glass which is your small model." — Koray Kavukcuoglu
"The user would assume, 'Oh, there must be some brilliant general-purpose AI behind the whole thing...' and now we actually built it. We built the general-purpose AI. It is one box." — Noam Shazeer
"I really didn't expect we could keep doing what we've been doing generation after generation, which is to pack the intelligence of Pro back into Flash." — Oriol Vinyals

Because if you go in a box and you try to hill-climb benchmarks, then you end up hill-climbing benchmarks and, you know, maybe you leaked your benchmark. It... it doesn't end up well. You don't want to build intelligence in a black box. You want it to be useful. You want people to be using it. Therefore, understanding what is required, like what is scratching the frontier, is both scratching the frontier of the research in terms of technical capability, but also scratching the surface of what is the next thing that you can enable for users. You can't do it if you don't actually do it with the products, and those two hand-in-hand define what the frontier means. So at the time, like Gemini starts, there are already a lot of machine learning models making it into products. And I think what seemed obvious is that if we create a single model more powerful than the average of the other models, like powering everything, that had to be like a leap forward. Whether there was a single product that could be created around that single, you know, model, maybe wasn't as clear at the time. But I think it was very clear that putting all the compute and intelligence into a single powerful model was going to leapfrog many things Google was already using machine learning for. And that was very exciting to be given that amount of compute and responsibility initially, but I think it's proven to be, indeed, kind of the... the core engine of Google intelligence. Even before we started the Gemini effort, there were a lot of people thinking about, you know, building incredibly general-purpose models that could do things. Oriol was leading some efforts in DeepMind, and I was sort of helping steer some efforts around the Pathways project and things like PaLM and PaLM 2 and so on. I actually said, "This is silly. We are fragmenting our efforts and fragmenting our compute." And if we're going to build an incredibly powerful model, we need to all come together and work on building a single model. That's actually where the name Gemini comes from, the twins. We mapped and then we reduced. Yeah, yeah, exactly. Something like that. I thought it was because I had twins. That too, that too. Jeff, that's a great... that's a great segue to sort of, again, going back to the formation of the Gemini project. I'm curious, how controversial was that? Obviously, as you say it now and we've done three and a half iterations, the sort of all the organizational complexity of bringing teams together is now sort of behind us. Was it like blatantly obvious at the time that we won't win and actually deliver on building the right product and model for our customers if we don't do this? Or did it sort of originate as a more pie-in-the-sky idea? I'm just curious, what was your level of confidence? I... I was sure the right thing was to come together. I actually articulated in a half-page memo like, "This is silly to fragment." Do we have this half-page? I'm not sure. We should share this half-page. We should put it somewhere or release it somewhere. But it felt like fragmenting our best ideas across different research teams that weren't really working together, and also fragmenting our compute. Just both of those issues seemed like things we should fix. It was a little bit organizationally complicated, and timezone-wise, there were lots of people in London and lots of people here. Eight hours apart is never a recipe for easy collaboration. But I think we've done a really good job of navigating that and bringing people together. Now we have a really good, amazing team all over the world, and we're cranking out good models. There were a bunch of teams building LLMs at the time that you just needed to mash together, basically. At some point, research in AI was a lot more academic, right? Like, you go back 10 years, it was a lot more academic research. And at that point, how you organize it is not really the key element. It is more about the exploration, and the speed of exploration is important. But as things get more and more focused, what you want is to really, as Jeff pointed out, have this focused operation. Rather than us trying to build things in parallel, because these things require a lot more focus and effort—and each one of them is like a major operation in terms of many researchers coming together and solving many problems—at that point, I think it was really, really a good idea to say, "Okay, this is the moment that we need to change." I think both organizations acted with great urgency on that and enabled it. I think that was an experience. Of course, it's never easy bringing two organizations together, but I think everyone realized that this is the right moment and there's a huge value to be gained from this. And I think all of us can see that the whole organization is very proud of what we have built together, right? Gemini is really the fruit of that. It's the scale. It's the fact that when you build one big, beautiful, giant LLM, it can do so many things. You really do need to put together that many people, that much compute, and infrastructure teams, data teams, and evals, and so on. Yeah, it's better to have one of those teams than five little ones. Yeah. One thing I would say is, from the start, we wanted Gemini to be... I mean, even pre-Gemini, one of the origins of the Pathways project was to explore a single model that could do many things. We wanted a multimodal model that could deal with all different modalities, a very large model that was sparse so you would activate different pieces of it for different kinds of things. And all three of those things are sort of represented in the Gemini models that we have today. I think now with Omni, we've gotten the multimodal aspects of it, and now we can even generate video. You want a model that is going to be able to roll forward that simulation, where the decisions coming out of the model are based on those future simulations. That's why I think Gemini Omni is a different category that is really transforming what we had with Gemini—which is mostly understanding and text output—and Veo, which is text input and video modeling, turning it into a really true world model. So we trained a 50-model ensemble for 300 million images, which was a lot at that time. They were 50 distinct ones, so we grouped the categories—like this one was going to be good at cars, and this one was going to be good at wild animals. Then we transferred the knowledge with distillation into a single model, and it was much more accurate than the single model you could have trained on the raw data. If you think about the glasses that were demonstrated at I/O, that's a product. But it's going to be made better because the models are better, they understand audio better, and they can speak to you better. That is a distinct product from Search. Exactly, right. I think it's clear to us there's definitely one model powering whatever it is. I'm not an expert, but as a user, sometimes I feel like I make an active choice of what I want to do with a digital device—like check my calendar, write an email, or buy something. Having that division might be more of a human factor rather than the technology being incapable of presenting all of this in a single product. But I feel like the choice of what I want to do, that focus... whether it goes away or we just evolve out of it, I'm not sure. I find myself liking the separation of concerns sometimes, so betting on one product is not something I would do at this time, at least for myself. We've been talking about informational products—products that deliver information. "But vector processing." But powered by things, you know, things like Omni. Maybe we will get into physical products in the future and start moving atoms and not just bits. 🎙️ Inside the Next Financial Crisis with Lloyd Blankfein #podcast #theforum

The Economic Club of New York(11.7K)·24:14·3.1K views·2026-05-29
Lloyd BlankfeinFormer Chairman and CEO, Goldman Sachs Becky QuickAnchor, CNBC
TL;DRLloyd Blankfein warns that a 15-year absence of a major financial reckoning has allowed overvalued, mismarked private assets to accumulate like "dry tinder" awaiting a spark.
However, he notes that the US economy remains highly resilient due to its flexible labor market,...

TL;DR

Lloyd Blankfein warns that a 15-year absence of a major financial reckoning has allowed overvalued, mismarked private assets to accumulate like "dry tinder" awaiting a spark. However, he notes that the US economy remains highly resilient due to its flexible labor market, and the Federal Reserve possesses stronger tools to manage a downturn today than it did in 2008.

Market Cycles, Animal Spirits, and the "Dry Tinder" Risk

  • The Inevitability of Financial Cycles: Market cycles are driven by the natural ebbing of traumatic memories. As the memory of the 2008 financial crisis fades, a new generation of market participants has become "sporty," taking on more risk and sowing the seeds for the next reckoning.
  • The "Forest Floor" Asset Risk: The primary risk in today's economy is not a specific catalyst, but rather the heavy accumulation of mismarked private assets on corporate balance sheets.
      • Private equity funds and insurance companies are holding onto aging assets despite record-high equity markets, indicating these assets are likely marked too high to attract buyers.
      • Blankfein compares this to dry timber on a forest floor. The eventual spark—whether a fat-finger error, a bad credit event, or a geopolitical shock—is inevitable and less important than the sheer volume of fuel waiting to burn.
  • The Need for Preventive "Brush Fires": Small, controlled market adjustments are healthy. While private credit is currently experiencing a mild, positive burnoff, the lack of a broader systemic reckoning over the last 15 years has prevented the natural clearing of bad assets from balance sheets.

The Shift to Private Credit and Structural Regulation Challenges

  • The Rise of Regulatory Arbitrage: Capital naturally migrates to areas with the fewest regulatory capital requirements, which has fueled the massive expansion of the unregulated private credit market.
  • The Fed's Coordination Problem: In 2008, the Federal Reserve could manage a systemic crisis by gathering representatives from just 5 to 16 major banks that held 75% to 90% of outstanding loans.
      • Today, due to the highly distributed nature of private credit, the Fed lacks both visibility and direct influence.
      • Blankfein notes that coordinating a similar workout today would require a venue the size of "Citi Field" rather than a single boardroom.
  • Secondary Market Pressure: Because private sponsors are delaying asset sales, limited partners (such as university endowments) are not receiving cash distributions. This has forced LPs to sell their limited partnership interests at a discount in secondary markets to raise capital.

Federal Reserve Tools and the National Debt Outlook

  • Stronger Crisis Defensive Tools: If a crisis occurs today, the US is in a much better position to respond than in 2008:
      • Banking Stability: Unlike the Great Financial Crisis, today's banking system is flush with capital and not in distress.
      • Rate Flexibility: With interest rates sitting between 3.75% and 4%, the Fed has significant room to cut rates, unlike the zero-rate environment of the post-2008 era.
  • How the US "Defaults" via Inflation: Because the US borrows in its own currency, it will not default nominally. Instead, default occurs when the government pays back creditors with devalued dollars that have lost their purchasing power.
  • The Importance of Fed Independence: The Federal Reserve is the ultimate institution defending the purchasing power of the dollar. Political assaults on Fed independence signal to global creditors that their investments are unsafe, which could trigger a sudden refusal to finance US debt or demand significantly higher interest rates.

US vs. European Economic Resilience and Social Contracts

  • Flexible Labor Markets Drive US Growth: The US economy's rapid recovery post-2008—contrasted with Europe's stagnant recovery—is a direct result of different national social contracts.
      • In the US, companies can rapidly downsize and restructure (e.g., Meta cutting its workforce by 8%), allowing labor and capital to be quickly recycled into more productive, high-growth industries.
  • European "Zombie" Companies: Europe’s social contract heavily protects existing jobs, making it incredibly difficult to lay off workers.
      • While this limits short-term unemployment shocks, it creates "zombie" companies, leaves mismarked assets on balance sheets, and ultimately leads to lower long-term growth and higher structural unemployment.
      • Though the US model leads to greater political polarization and social friction, it fosters unmatched innovation, resilience, and economic speed.

Notable Quotes

"People with P&Ls can't walk away from their... you know, can't deny what they had thought in the past. And so, I think we have a system that allows for animal spirits, and with that comes the inevitability... I wish it would never go off the rails. But if you're going to have animal spirits... that's going to produce a kind of cycle." — Lloyd Blankfein
"I'm more worried about the kindling than I am about the cigarette, you know, the loose cigarette butt at this point. That's what I would work on if I were, you know, a regulator today. I'd be worried about getting rid of all the mismarked assets here." — Lloyd Blankfein
"How does a country default when it borrows money in its own currency? The safety of that... that our creditors look to to defend the purchasing power of the dollar, is the Fed. And so, this kind of an assault on the independence of the Fed, that just makes our creditors think that they're going to get defaulted to." — Lloyd Blankfein
I'm Becky Quick of CNBC and your host of The Forum. I'll be guiding you through exclusive conversations among some of the world's global leaders. But I do think that Wall Street does a very good job of responding to the uncertainties that exist at every moment about what is going to happen next, being good contingency planners, being very resilient, and being great responders. Of course, they get killed by the reviewers and the pundits who have after-acquired information to look back and say, "Aha, that's not what I would have done." With dimmer memories, people retiring, and new people coming in who only read about the financial crisis or the tech bubble in books, it doesn't quite register. People get sportier and sportier, which then sows the seeds for the next crisis and the next reckoning. As we separate ourselves from the trauma—and we haven't really had one for more than 15 years since the Great Financial Crisis—things get a little more lax. It's just, again, the nature of things. And the real issue for me in the market today is that we haven't had a reckoning in a long time, so we haven't cleared those assets that are on balance sheets that probably are marked too high. People thought they were worth X, and they may not be worth X. Why do I say that? Because a lot of these private assets were acquired on the balance sheets of private equity funds, insurance companies bought them, and the companies that do this activity are in business to recycle them and sell them as soon as they can. We've just had the highest equity markets ever and the best financing markets ever, yet assets are getting kind of aged on balance sheets. So that must mean that people who are highly motivated to sell them are not selling them, because the price isn't being met, probably because they're marked too high. So, the metaphor that I would use is that we've been accumulating a lot of dry tinder on the floor of the forest. If you think of California and when it gets dry, they put out the fire alerts because it's dry outside. The real problem is the fuel on the floor of the forest: the tinder, the dried wood that fell down. And at some point, inevitably, someone will toss a cigarette butt, someone will be careless with a campfire, or there'll be a lightning strike. Do you blame the lightning, or do you blame the cigarette? It's inevitable. It's really the accumulation of the debris, the fuel, that makes this inevitable at some point. I think, because we have not had a reckoning, we've not had those preventive brush fires that they try to do in a controlled way to burn that stuff off. We are having one now, a little bit, in private credit, which is a good thing. You want these little things; markets pay attention to little things and get them out of the way before they engulf and get out of control. That's a little bit like a controlled burnoff. But because we haven't had that in a big sense in a long time, I'm nervous it will be something. It could be a fat finger. It could be somebody saying, "Oh my god, the price of oil is going to be sustained this high." It could be a lightning strike, or it could be a meteor hitting the planet. Something will happen that, at a different time, would not have set anything off because there wouldn't have been a lot to set off. But because that kindling is there, something like that will happen. It could be a credit event, like some highly leveraged fund being unable to make a payment. It could be an insurance regulator that looks at the balance sheet. Don't forget, insurance is regulated by 50 state regulators, not necessarily all of the same caliber. They may look at the balance sheet of an insurance company and say, "Gee, we don't know if these assets are going to be sustained. We're going to make you sell some." Then they might find themselves selling into a market that doesn't want them and won't pay the price, and that might make everybody crazy at the same time, trying to go for a door to sell stuff that no one wants. I've lived through a lot of these. It could be almost anything, and it could be something that at a different time wouldn't have registered. The problem is the accumulation of assets on balance sheets that may not be worth what they are marked. Well, Goldman Sachs, prior to 2008, was not regulated as a bank holding company. There's always regulatory arbitrage, just like there's any other arbitrage in the market. Capital will find places where it is cheapest. Capital is going to be cheap where there are fewer regulatory requirements that you hold capital against leverage, and so there'll always be stuff like this. As I said, even Goldman Sachs, in its regulated form, has funds that are private credit funds because it's other people's money in an account not subject to regulation, not on the balance sheet of the firm. In fact, ours were very big, very early. So, I think that that's a part of the market. It creates a problem in the system. Again, it might not be the spark, but it's part of the potential. In the financial crisis, the Fed could get probably 90% of all the outstanding loans around its conference room at the Federal Reserve building if they just had 16 banks represented. We're the biggest economy, yet we move the fastest, we're the most resilient, and we're the most likely to foster innovation. Theirs is kind of stickier. In the short term, they probably have less unemployment than they otherwise would, but over time, they have less efficient, worse-performing businesses, and therefore lower growth and lower employment over the long run. JPMorgan Chase CEO Jamie Dimon speaks at Reagan National Economic Forum — 5/29/2026

CNBC Television(3.4M)·39:22·15.9K views·2026-05-29
Jamie DimonCEO, JPMorgan Chase Morgan BrennanAnchor, CNBC
TL;DRJPMorgan Chase CEO Jamie Dimon warns that shifting global geopolitics and domestic bureaucratic inertia are the greatest threats to Western democracy, urging the U.S.
to deploy "smart industrial policy" to secure critical supply chains, enact aggressive regulatory reforms...

TL;DR

JPMorgan Chase CEO Jamie Dimon warns that shifting global geopolitics and domestic bureaucratic inertia are the greatest threats to Western democracy, urging the U.S. to deploy "smart industrial policy" to secure critical supply chains, enact aggressive regulatory reforms to spark GDP growth, and take a decisive stance against foreign adversaries.

Geopolitics, Alliances, and Dollar Dominance

  • Shifting Tectonic Plates: Dimon emphasizes that escalating geopolitical tensions—including the war in Ukraine, conflicts in the Middle East, and the actions of the "evil axis" (Russia, Iran, and North Korea)—completely dwarf short-term economic forecasts and threaten the future of the free world.
  • The Threat of Iran: Dimon advocates for a more aggressive foreign policy stance, stating that the U.S. should have taken decisive coalition action against Iran decades ago to prevent state-sponsored terrorism.
      • Nuclear Proliferation: He warns that if Iran successfully acquires nuclear weapons, it will trigger rapid nuclear proliferation across Egypt, Saudi Arabia, the UAE, Kuwait, South Korea, the Philippines, Japan, and Australia.
  • Economic Realpolitik and Dollar Dominance: The U.S. dollar's status as the global reserve currency is directly dependent on the U.S. maintaining both the preeminent military and the preeminent economy in the world.
  • Strengthening Western Alliances: Dimon suggests the Western world must deep-link its economic relationships to counter adversaries like China.
      • European Trade: He proposes that the U.S. should offer Europe a major free trade agreement conditional on structural economic reforms (referencing the Draghi report).
      • North American Integration: He urges a swift and favorable resolution to USMCA negotiations, highlighting that Canada and Mexico share a vital, common economic system with the U.S. that includes critical resources like oil, gas, lithium, and potash.

The $1.5T Security and Resiliency Initiative

  • Direct Capital Injection: Under Dimon's leadership, JPMorgan Chase launched a $1.5 trillion, 10-year Security and Resiliency Initiative to finance domestic and allied capabilities in critical sectors. The firm expects to exceed this $1.5 trillion target.
  • Strategic Focus Sectors: The initiative focuses on securing supply chains in key areas, including:
      • Military equipment, drones, and missiles.
      • Active pharmaceutical ingredients (APIs) and semiconductors.
      • Rare earth elements and advanced manufacturing.
      • Liquefied natural gas (LNG) infrastructure to support European allies.
  • Smart Industrial Policy: Dimon advocates for targeted government intervention to rebuild critical supply chains, rejecting the idea that this constitutes "state capitalism."
      • Rare Earths Example: The U.S. currently imports only $200 million in rare earth elements. Dimon argues that implementing high tariffs or unilateral import bans on Chinese rare earths would force domestic resiliency. While this might slightly raise costs (e.g., adding $100 to the price of a car), it would achieve 100% supply chain security.

Domestic Reform and Reigniting the "American Dream"

  • The Permitting Crisis: Dimon criticizes U.S. regulatory and permitting delays as a "disgrace," noting that infrastructure projects like rebuilding the Baltimore bridge are mired in bureaucracy.
      • "Free" Growth Policy: He challenges Congress to spend six months passing cost-free regulatory and permitting reforms (without debating revenues or expenses) to boost GDP growth by an estimated 1%.
  • Supporting the Bottom 30%: While the top 70% of Americans are thriving, the bottom 30% have experienced 25 years of wage stagnation. Dimon asserts that throwing federal money at the problem has failed, and solutions must focus on local policies regarding housing, crime, and education.
  • Tax Policy and Earned Income Tax Credit (EITC): Aligning with Jeff Bezos’s recent comments that the government has a spending problem rather than a revenue problem, Dimon champions the expansion of the EITC.
      • EITC Proposal: He recommends expanding the credit to single workers earning $12,000–$14,000 and removing the child requirement. This $50 billion expansion would encourage workforce entry, reduce crime and suicide rates, and pay for itself through improved social and economic outcomes.
  • JPMorgan's "American Dream Initiative": The bank is expanding branches in low-income neighborhoods, teaching financial literacy, and scaling its small business banking footprint with the goal of growing from 7 million to 10 million small business clients.

Monetary Policy, Inflation, and Market Risks

  • The Fed Under Kevin Warsh: Dimon welcomes newly appointed Federal Reserve Chair Kevin Warsh, praising his market experience and fresh perspective.
      • Regulatory Rollback: Dimon supports Warsh's intent to reduce the Fed’s balance sheet, scale back quantitative easing (QE), and steer the Fed away from non-monetary issues like climate change and DEI, allowing the OCC to handle primary bank regulation.
  • Structural Inflationary Forces: Dimon believes inflation is far stickier than the market assumes. Ongoing inflationary pressures include:
      • Global remilitarization and the restructuring of global trade.
      • Heavy spending on AI infrastructure.
      • Domestic immigration limits.
      • The cumulative effect of consecutive $2 trillion annual federal deficits.
  • Market Exuberance and Credit Cycle Concerns: Dimon views current equity and debt markets as highly exuberant, driven by AI hype. While JPMorgan is prepared for interest rates ranging from 2% to 8%, Dimon warns that low credit spreads present a real risk.
      • Private Credit: He does not view the $1.7 trillion private credit market as a systemic threat, but expects weak underwriting standards and a lack of transparency to result in worse-than-expected credit performance during the next downturn.

JPMorgan Strategy and M&A

  • Strategic Acquisitions: Dimon confirms JPMorgan is open to deploying its excess capital toward acquisitions in the $10 billion to $20 billion range to enhance its capabilities in payments, asset management, and capital raising.
  • Competitive Focus: Rather than focusing on its current market leadership, JPMorgan's management is focused on areas where competitors are winning, studying platforms like Citadel, Stripe, and Revolut to improve the bank's own systems and digital offerings.

The tax policy piece of this, specifically: last week Jeff Bezos was on CNBC and made a lot of news, basically said bottom 50% of Americans should not pay federal income tax. I like, and, the, the earned income tax credit, which today, as it stands, if you're a single parent making $14,000, the government gives you seven. At the end of the year, in a check, you know, some people are entitled to it and don't get it. If you have no children, they give you like 700 bucks. Jobs lead to, the money would go to the person, so it's not big, big government design where they decide if you can buy potato chips and soda or not. They would spend it on their families and their homes, they spend it locally, and might foster local restaurants, they might buy more insurance or a tutor for the kids or something like that. And it would lift up those economies, but it also has the great virtue of bringing people into the workforce. Making work pay more. And we know one thing about jobs: better social outcome, more household formation, less crime, less suicide, less recidivism. So if you actually do the full analysis, which is hard to do, and we really haven't, I guarantee it'd more than pay for itself. And that would cost like another 50 billion dollars. If they did that, yeah, I think people would be willing to pay as long as it went to them. How do you see this new regime taking shape, especially when you have a bond market that, according to some investors, is pricing in a Warsh effect? Is... Oh, yeah, I don't, I'm not sure that's true. So first of all, high-quality guy. Experienced, knows markets, was at the central bank, and he was at the New York Fed when we were going through the Great Financial Crisis. It's, it's a fresh look, and it's good. I like his views. I mean, the, the, the Federal Reserve did in fact get involved in a lot of stuff that they should not have, in my view. And they're independent on monetary policy, they're not independent on regulatory policy. And when they did regulatory policy, they violated the Constitution. There were no cost-benefit studies, no analysis, no other stuff they're supposed to do. And, you know, that, that was wrong. You know, and I'm not, and I like Jay Powell too. I'm not just saying that's, you, when I look at my own institution, I often am saying the same thing: "you know what? In hindsight, we shouldn't have done that." And so, I think they should take a fresh look. They were the adults in the town, they still are, but they're better off looking at the financial system as a whole, let the OCC do more of the regulating of the banks, reduce all this, you know. They, they were getting into climate and DEI and, and stuff like that. We've been adding year after year after year, you wouldn't know this, rules and regulations on top of rules and regulations. I think you can make the system safer and let banks do what they're supposed to do: make more loans, create more liquidity, and have more intervention in the markets by the people who should do it. And the Fed put themselves in a position where if there's any kerfuffle, they have to do something. I think that's a huge mistake. So I think he's right about relooking at that. And I think he's right about reducing the size of their balance sheet. You know, we had too much QE, and we still don't fully know the effect of 10 trillion dollars of borrowing and 8 trillion dollars of QE. My view, when I, when we talk about regulations, is: we should talk about better. I think you can let banks lend more, create more liquidity, and make them safer. LIVE: Andrew Bailey speaks at Reykjavik 2026 economic conference

The Sun(6.5M)·259:50·1.3K views·2026-05-29

TL;DRAt the Reykjavik economic conference, central bankers from the UK and US emphasized that persistent inflation—exacerbated by Middle East energy shocks and structural shifts like AI and aging demographics—requires highly flexible, state-contingent…
monetary frameworks and...

TL;DR

At the Reykjavik economic conference, central bankers from the UK and US emphasized that persistent inflation—exacerbated by Middle East energy shocks and structural shifts like AI and aging demographics—requires highly flexible, state-contingent monetary frameworks and prolonged "higher-for-longer" interest rate environments.

Geopolitical Energy Shocks and the Bank of England's Response

  • UK Inflation Miss: Bank of England (BoE) Governor Andrew Bailey reported that UK inflation hit 2.8% in April, missing the 2% target by 0.8 percentage points due to fuel and household energy price spikes caused by the Middle East conflict.
  • The "Lambda" ($\lambda$) Policy Trade-off: Bailey detailed how the BoE manages the trade-off between inflation and output volatility using constrained discretion:
    • Direct Effects (High $\lambda$): Monetary policy should "look through" temporary, direct energy price spikes to avoid unnecessary output volatility.
    • Indirect Effects (Moderate $\lambda$): A more active response is required to prevent protracted indirect costs from keeping inflation elevated.
    • Second-Round Effects (Low $\lambda$): Tolerance is minimal if wage-price spirals or elevated inflation expectations begin to take root.
  • Scenarios Replacing Central Projections: Due to extreme unpredictability in the Middle East, the BoE has suspended its standard "central projection" in favor of three distinct economic scenarios (A, B, and C) to guide policy decisions.
  • Rate Decision and Financial Impact: The BoE made an active choice to hold its policy rate at 3.75%, taking rate cuts off the table for the immediate future.
    • Mortgage Rate Hikes: Key quoted rates for 2-year and 5-year fixed mortgages in the UK have risen by approximately 1% since the conflict began.
    • Labor Market Shift: Unlike the highly tight and hard-to-read labor market of 2022, current data shows a gradually and predictably softening UK labor market.

Regional Fed Insights: Oil, AI, and Demographics

  • The Power of Regionalism: Jeffrey Smith, President of the Federal Reserve Bank of Kansas City, highlighted that the Fed’s 12-district system prevents monetary policy from being dominated solely by Wall Street and Washington.
  • US Energy Efficiency: Although Middle East tensions have impaired roughly 10% of global oil production, the US economy is far more resilient to oil shocks than it was in the 1970s:
    • Efficiency Gains: The US economy requires only 3,000 BTUs of energy to produce $1 of GDP today, compared to 12,000 BTUs in the 1970s.
    • Terms of Trade: Thanks to the shale revolution, the US is now a net oil exporter, meaning wealth transfers from higher prices remain domestic rather than flowing abroad.
  • The Looming Demographic Shift: The US is facing a major demographic inflection point, with the population aged 75 and older set to double from 6% to 12% between 2020 and 2040.
    • Healthcare Jobs Surge: The healthcare and social assistance sector added 650,000 jobs over a 12-month period, while non-healthcare employment actually contracted.
    • Downward Demographics Risk: Rising demand for elder-care aids could pull workers from the child-care sector, driving up child-care costs and creating a negative demographic loop.
  • AI and the Labor Force: Smith noted that AI is not actively replacing workers or depressing hiring. Instead, businesses are leveraging AI, automation, and robotics to offset the rapid attrition of retiring baby boomers (roughly 300,000 workers aging out of the workforce monthly).

Formulating a Flexible Monetary Framework at the Federal Reserve

  • The Dual Mandate Balance: Fed Vice Chair for Supervision Michelle Bowman outlined a flexible framework that places equal weight on price stability and maximum employment, arguing that policymakers must focus on whichever objective deviates furthest from its target.
  • Key Policy Indicators: Bowman monitors private domestic final purchases (excluding volatile components of GDP) to gauge true aggregate demand, alongside average payroll gains and core PCE inflation (excluding temporary tariff effects).
  • Decisions on Rate Hikes and Cuts: Bowman explained the logic behind her historical and potential future votes:
    • Rate Hikes (2022–2023): Strongly supported aggressive tightening when demand-side stimulus and post-pandemic supply constraints drove US inflation to a 40-year high.
    • Rate Cuts (Late 2025): Dissented in July 2025 to advocate for a proactive 25 basis point cut to protect a fragile labor market, subsequently supporting 75 basis points in cumulative cuts to ease the policy stance from restrictive toward neutral.
  • Current US Economic Health: Real GDP continues to grow at a moderate-to-solid pace, supported by heavy AI-related business investments. However, the labor market remains vulnerable with unemployment at 4.3% (as of April), and energy-driven price increases require continued vigilance.

Notable Quotes

"Regardless of what happens, the task of monetary policy is to ensure that as the adjustment to the shock happens, inflation does not become embedded." — Andrew Bailey, Governor of the Bank of England
"I place little stock in assuming that the most recent runup in prices is transitory within an acceptable time horizon. As such, my focus remains strongly on inflation and setting the correct course for policy." — Jeffrey Smith, President & CEO of the Federal Reserve Bank of Kansas City
"Our dual mandate places equal weight on maximum employment and price stability. Consequently, when there's tension in achieving the two objectives, in my view, it's important not to favor one side of the mandate over the other." — Michelle Bowman, Vice Chair for Supervision at the Federal Reserve Board
...ation at the Bank of England, where he was responsible for the supervision of banks and financial institutions. He has also held numerous senior roles within the bank, including work on financial stability, international economics, and crisis management. In addition to his role as a governor, he now serves as chair of the Financial Stability Board, placing him at the center of global efforts to safeguard the financial system. We will have a Q&A session with him moderated by Stephanie Flanders from Bloomberg. Stephanie is one of the most experienced and respected economic commentators globally, with a career spanning journalism, markets, and policymaking, from the BBC to JP Morgan, and now the head of economics and government at Bloomberg, where she leads the global team covering economic development in real time, overseeing the research and journalism of 250 economists and reporters worldwide and hosting a weekly podcast, *Trumponomics*. Governor Bailey, we are very pleased to have you here with us. Welcome. Thank you. Well, good morning. It's a great pleasure to be back in Reykjavik for this excellent conference. As you know, my ancestors were in the chocolate-making business—line of business. So, I can't match you for either the sense of adventure or the sense of risk, I'm afraid. You take the prize for that; that's truly impressive. So, when we were here last year and I spoke, I set out how at the Bank of England we were building scenarios into our monetary policy processes in response to the review that Ben Bernanke did of our forecasting, and particularly in response to the review as he undertook the review on the significant uncertainty around the COVID period. And I said that these scenarios, I anticipated, would help us to explore what might happen if a particular shock were to hit the economy, how a given set of shocks might propagate through to inflation depending on the strengths of different economic mechanisms, and how monetary policy might respond in different states of the world. The use of scenarios, I concluded, would help us build resilience into our inflation-targeting regime and help to secure the nominal anchor in a world prone to big shocks to supply as well as to demand. Well, it's good to have foresight occasionally. I didn't have that much foresight a year ago as to what was going to happen, I will openly admit. But of course, for all of us, the conflict in the Middle East has led to sharp increases in global oil and gas prices as transit through the Strait of Hormuz has been curtailed and critical infrastructure has been damaged in the region or destroyed. And this is a very big negative supply shock to the world economy. In the United Kingdom, the outlook for inflation has been significantly affected. Before hostilities broke out in February, UK inflation was on track to fall to around the 2% target from April. The task for monetary policy in my view would have been to keep it there, with underlying disinflation, building slack in the real economy, and a softening labor market. Some further easing of monetary policy was on the cards. Well, if you detect a note of frustration in my comments, you will be right. Because instead, of course, we had an inflation number for April that came in at 2.8% last week. We think that 0.8 percentage points of upside news to household energy prices relative to our pre-conflict expectations, which is driven really by fuel prices, is a reflection of those events that overtook us. And in reality, inflation is likely to go higher over this year as utility bills rise and firms pass higher costs through supply chains. And of course, there's nothing monetary policy can do to prevent higher energy prices from affecting businesses and households. For net importers of energy like the UK, the terms of trade have worsened and real incomes will fall. The shock will push up inflation and weigh on activity. How much it will do that will depend on how the situation in the Middle East evolves. A resolution at the source, which would be by far the best outcome, a reopening of the Strait of Hormuz, and repairing the damage that has been done to the world's energy infrastructure can reduce the impact of the negative shock to our economies. Equally, the longer the conflict continues, the worse the impact on our economies will become. But regardless of what happens, the task of monetary policy is to ensure that as the adjustment to the shock happens, inflation does not become embedded. And in the United Kingdom, that means returning 12-month CPI to our 2% target in the medium term. Now, since our founding in 1694, the Bank of England has worked to promote the public good and benefit to our people. That's our objective, set out in our founding charter. Exactly what that means has evolved over the centuries. Suffice to say that since the Bank of England Act of 1998 in respect of monetary policy, the Bank has operated independently of government with a clearly defined objective to maintain price stability. And this objective is further defined in the annual remit letters from the government to the Bank's Monetary Policy Committee. In turn, the MPC is accountable to Parliament for its actions—for the people and Parliament. Delegation to an independent central bank in this way works as a commitment device to reduce incentives to reneg on the promise of price stability under the pressures of daily politics. For monetary policy, the temptation previously was always to tolerate a little more inflation in the short run to support the real economy. But as economic history has shown, if it's likely that policymakers will deliberately reneg on the promise of low and stable inflation, people will factor that into their expectations, driving up inflation without any benefit to the real economy. The promise of an independent monetary policy is that it will not succumb to such temptations. And that's why in our remit, it is clear that the 2% inflation target applies at all times. The remit recognizes, however, that attempting to bring inflation back to the target too quickly may cause undesirable volatility in output. In some instances, monetary policy has to manage a trade-off between the speed with which it brings inflation back to target and the consideration that should be placed on the variability of output. And in this sense, the UK inflation target, like others around the world, is flexible rather than strict. But of course, the question that begs is exactly how flexible should it be? While the MPC's remit specifies the inflation target explicitly as 2%, it provides no explicit guidance on the precise weight that should be assigned to output relative to inflation. In the common jargon of monetary economists and central bankers, the remit leaves the value of the parameter lambda unspecified, except perhaps implying that it should be strictly positive. So, put that way, the remit can be regarded as an incomplete contract between the government and the MPC. But I would argue that's a feature, not a bug. From the point of view of Parliament, the remit is a commitment device, but it's one that leaves the MPC with constrained discretion. This is consistent with the view that the appropriate way of managing a trade-off is state- and shock-dependent, and so is not something that can be predetermined. For example, to the extent that it's appropriate to look through temporary price level shocks, the appropriate value of lambda—the weight on output when managing the trade-off when such shocks arrive—may be high. Equally, in the face of risks to the anchoring of inflation expectations, a very low value of lambda may be more appropriate. On this view, the remit encodes delegation of lambda selection to the MPC depending on the situation that it faces. The remit recognizes that there are times when the MPC is likely to be faced with more significant trade-offs that need to be managed, but it also prescribes additional public scrutiny in those circumstances. Whenever inflation moves away from the target by more than 1%, the Governor of the Bank is required to send an open letter to the government—to the Chancellor of the Exchequer, that is—to set out the outlook for inflation and the action the Monetary Policy Committee is taking to return inflation to the 2% target. And in response, the Chancellor can opine on whether the government views the balance that the MPC is striking in managing any trade-off as appropriate or otherwise. Now, alongside my remarks today, we've published an article on the Bank of England website describing these issues in much greater detail. But let me add here that any monetary policy regime involves constrained discretion in some form. Even under the gold standard, when central banks were highly constrained by the task to maintain the convertibility of their currencies into gold, managing the system required considerable discretion at quite a number of times. In the 19th century, for example, the Bank of England had to manage a trade-off between convertibility and the needs of commerce and trade. And there's a long history of debate, familiar to people in this room, I know, about how much monetary policy should be constrained by rules and how much discretion should be left to central bankers. Reflecting that debate, different jurisdictions have arrived at somewhat different answers to how constrained discretion should operate when you're in an inflation-targeting framework. For example, the Bank of England's monetary policy remit bears many similarities to frameworks with a dual mandate. Given the widely held view that money is neutral in the long run, both involve a focus on inflation stability in the long run. In the short run, both involve balancing inflation volatility with a concern for output volatility. And both lend themselves to be formalized in terms of a quadratic loss function, which is where the Greek letter lambda comes into the story, of course. But there are subtle differences between these two, however, and I would highlight two. The first is the clarity with which the remit acknowledges that achieving the inflation target is the best and only contribution that monetary policy can make in the long run. The primacy of price stability in the MPC's remit makes clear that there are times when the focus should be squarely on returning inflation to target, and there are limits to how high lambda should be. In that sense, our remit is more constrained. But second, and subject to the first, the MPC's remit affords more flexibility over how management of a trade-off should be approached than a dual mandate, which almost by definition can be taken to suggest that a balanced approach should be taken. I would argue that this additional flexibility that we have can be useful in allowing monetary policy to approach trade-offs in different ways depending on the circumstances. Explicit language on how trade-offs should be managed gives clear directions on how the choice should be approached, however, and a clear framework for accountability to Parliament. So, that begs the question, of course: how does the MPC's remit suggest we should respond to the recent rise in energy prices caused by the conflict in the Middle East? Well, monetary policy generally looks through the direct effects of energy prices on inflation. It takes time for changes in interest rates to affect the economy and inflation. So, higher interest rates might only push inflation below target once the energy price shock has passed, resulting in undesirable volatility in both inflation and activity. And even if the lags in the effects of interest rate changes were shorter, offsetting the direct effect of an energy price shock would require pushing down core inflation by generating additional slack in the economy. Looking through the direct effects of an energy shock avoids such output volatility, as the MPC's remit calls for. In other words, a higher value of lambda is appropriate with respect to the direct effects of an energy price shock. Because they take longer to come through, the argument for looking through the indirect effects is weaker, and protracted indirect effects could keep inflation above target for too long unless monetary policy responds. This suggests a moderate value of lambda for the indirect effects. By leaning against indirect effects, monetary policy may also reduce the risk that higher inflation becomes embedded through higher inflation expectations and second-round effects. Such effects could arise, for example, if a rise in inflation expectations leads workers to bargain more strongly for wage increases and firms raise wages to maintain real pay for their employees, which in turn would increase their costs and could lead them to set higher prices. Monetary policy should not look through such effects. For second-round effects, the appropriate value of lambda is low, consistent with the primacy of price stability in the remit. But the size, of course, of any second-round effects, in addition to the direct and indirect effects, is highly uncertain. And in addition to being highly uncertain, second-round effects build up more slowly than direct and indirect effects. And that leaves monetary policymakers with a difficult judgment call. Because interest rate changes take time to take their effect, we can't wait for conclusive evidence of the strength of the second-round effects. But responding too early may generate undesirable volatility in output, and inflation may drop below the 2% target in the medium term if second-round effects turn out to be smaller than anticipated. I would argue this speaks to the desirability of a state-contingent approach, including with respect to the choice of lambda. Given the context of softness in the real economy and uncertainty around the scale and duration of the shock, tolerating temporarily above-target inflation to provide some support for the real economy is an appropriate way to approach the trade-off. But that tolerance would weaken if signs of second-round effects begin to emerge. So, this takes me back to the scenarios. In the monetary policy report that we published at the end of April, we presented three scenarios—A, B, and C—for possible outcomes for the UK economy and inflation over the next three years, without any one of them being designated as a central projection as we normally would do, and without assigning any probability weights to them. This is not because we expect this to be the norm for the future. Rather, it reflects the uncertainty we face with the unpredictability of the situation in the Middle East. As and when we can be more confident about how world events will unfold, we will return to presenting a central projection—one that the MPC agrees is a reasonable baseline—using scenarios as vehicles then for exploring the inevitable risks around it and exploring differences of views within the committee. But for now, the scenarios, we think, give us a better way to think about the policy discussion. The reality of the situation we are in is that there is a range of possible outcomes, and we have to be ready to respond to all of them. Using scenarios like this is part of being ready to make that response, whatever happens, and it allows us to set policy now with a clear sense of the risks we have to balance. The scenarios are designed to capture the key questions that are relevant to the policy discussion. How long will the conflict go on? What will be the scale of the effects on energy prices of the conflict? And particularly important for us, could it lead to second-round effects and inflation persistence? The scenarios do not pretend to be exhaustive of what could happen. They're meant to illustrate somewhere around the range of possible outcomes. While we are ready to adjust course as needed depending on how the situation evolves, our decision to hold the policy rate at 3.75% at our most recent meeting was an active choice given the range of possible outcomes. Having taken expected cuts off the table for now, we already tightened policy considerably in response to the shock relative to what had been expected by markets. And that's already affecting financial conditions and the economy. Key quoted rates on mortgages have increased since the onset of the conflict. So, the decision to hold reflected a judgment that continued weakness in UK activity and the labor market is likely to lessen the strength of the second-round effects from higher energy prices, while recognizing that these effects are likely to be stronger the larger and more persistent is the rise in global energy prices. Uncertainty about the strength of the second-round effects means that monetary policy needs to balance the costs of leaning too little against these effects against the cost of responding too much. And the right balance is likely to change depending on how events unfold and how the economy reacts. To state the obvious, that means, of course, that we have to monitor very closely the situation in the Middle East and how it affects the UK economy and inflation, and adjust policy as required. But whatever happens, the use of scenarios and the flexibility that we've built into our processes following the Bernanke review has prepared us well for responding to events as they unfold and for returning inflation to the 2% target in line with the remit and for the good of the people of the United Kingdom. Thank you. We got a big choice of chairs. Thank you very much, Governor. That was great. You talked a lot about the importance of discretion, and I guess the other way—the other way of thinking about discretion, or another way of putting it, is that it's just the ability to respond to different situations in a different way. Yeah. Of course, looking at the UK, what's happened in Europe, 2022 also saw an energy shock, also saw rising inflation expectations. How different is the current situation? That's a great question. Yeah, I think there are several differences that are important here. One is to remember that the energy shock in 2022 came in two parts. So, we had a February 2022 initial outbreak of the conflict in Ukraine, and then we had... the bigger one came in around August, really, actually. So, it had a different characteristic. But I think the bigger point I would make is the legacy of COVID and the problem—and I look back on this particularly sharply now—the problem we had, I think, was judging how tight particularly the labor market was. Every forecast that I know—and we publish a summary of outside forecasts in every monetary policy report we publish—had unemployment going up in the UK. This includes our forecast, by the way, when the so-called furlough scheme came to an end. In other words, when the state paying for people to stay in jobs but not be at work came to an end, we all thought there was going to be... I think I got asked repeatedly, "How much scarring do you think there's going to be?" And we all thought there would be, and we all thought there would be an increase in unemployment. I mean, the furlough scheme was a great scheme in many respects, but it obscured the read of the labor market, I think. So, of course, what happened was exactly the opposite. We had no increase in unemployment. The labor market became tighter, and it became very clear talking to companies that they were, quote, "hoarding labor" deliberately—that they were worried about if they lost people, they wouldn't be able to get them back. Now, I think today we're obviously not in that position. I mean, there's always an interesting task with reading the labor market, but I don't think we're in that level of uncertainty. The picture of a gradually softening labor market comes through pretty consistently. And is it... obviously, you had that experience, you learned—some would say, you learned quite a few lessons from that experience because the labor market was not as we thought, as many people thought. There are a lot of questions now around UK credibility, both on inflation given the recent record, but also clearly the political uncertainty which we're seeing in market pricing, particularly in bond markets, but also in inflation expectations. Is it harder for you to be using that discretion in this quite different situation, given that sort of legacy that you're carrying? I think the legacy makes it a bit harder because, if you look on the other side of the picture to the sort of the softening labor market, softening activity, going the other way—put on the other side of the balance—would be inflation expectations. And, you know, I think we're very aware that the legacy of four years ago does weigh in people's assessment of what inflation expectations are in response to the shock we're having at the moment. So, I don't think we should be surprised that there's some element of what you might call a hangover from that period. But of course, we have to take that very seriously into consideration, because it's real. But does the change in inflation expectations mean something different this time because of the state, because of what's in the labor market? Well, I think that's where it gets really interesting, because I think that's a judgment that weighs very heavily. You're precisely right, because what you can end up with is somewhat more elevated inflation expectations, but it isn't coming through in wage expectations when they're measured, and it isn't coming through in wage settlements at the moment, particularly in the private sector in the UK. So again, that's where, as I said in my remarks, judgment is absolutely at the center of what we do. And I think that judgment between those two things—what weight do we put on the read from inflation expectations versus labor market expectations—is very important. And I guess that means, at least on the outside, it sort of seems that you wouldn't only be looking at inflation expectations as a clue to second-round effects, which is obviously the thing you're worried about. So, if it's not that, what other indicators are you going to be looking at? So, we look at—I mean, we look at a whole series. We're not going to be specific about a particular indicator. I mean, there are elements of the inflation series, because I think things like services inflation give you some reading on the sort of underlying state of the domestic economy, more so than some of the other bits of the index. The labor market will be another. The only problem in the UK we'll have is that there is a distinct seasonality to private sector wage settlements, and we've passed that season before the shock happens, so we don't get as much information for the remainder of this calendar year on— And that involves waiting quite a long time. Yes, and that's the challenge. Going back to what I was saying, the other thing we do is we spend a lot of time—I spend a lot of time—going around the country talking to companies, and we are doing a lot of that. And we come into this with companies telling us quite consistently, before the shock happened, that they were finding it hard to pass costs into prices. So, if you think back to sort of events that happened, you know, over a year ago, if you think back to things like the national insurance change, minimum wage changes, companies were telling us quite consistently, "It is getting much harder to pass those through." And I think probably by the end of last year, we were concluding that a bit more of it was coming through in reduced margins. So, the implication of what you were saying about a legacy is obviously you feel conscious of that trade-off between wanting to be seen to be responding quickly but waiting for those second-round effects. Has the bond market tightening actually bought you some time on that? It probably has, but there's a number of things going on there, I think. So, as I said in the remarks, I mean, one of the things... we have in effect tightened policy in my view now. This does depend—you'll get different answers, you know, from different members of the committee on this because it depends what you thought you were going to do for the rest of this year before any of this happened. I'd been quite clear I thought we probably would cut rates once or twice this year. That's off the table. So, we've had, as I said in my remarks, about a 1% increase in mortgage rates for new mortgages—so, for two- and five-year fixed-rate mortgages. That's obviously a tightening of financial conditions, so that's real. I think the other thing we've had is that the UK bond market has responded somewhat more, and this is linked because I think we've seen this very big change in the makeup of government bond markets, including our own. So, there was, I think, more than you would see historically, positioning particularly in the sort of the hedge fund world on the basis that we were going to cut, and that positioning has come off largely. So, you had a bigger sort of reversal of positioning going on, and I think that also has had some effect on the UK curve. It's coming down now progressively over the last couple of weeks. You know, I hope it goes on, but I think that will depend on events in the Middle East. Does the UK... we're running out of time, but does the UK have a problem here? And is it a puzzle for the Bank of England? I mean, the neutral rate, the rate at which we can sort of not have a problem with inflation, just seems to be meaningfully higher than you might have expected. I mean, this is one of the great puzzles, I think, that we all spend quite a bit of time on, as to how you square neutral rates. We do have a more open economy; that's one of the sort of arguments we come back to eventually. So, that may have some bearing on the question. But it is something that we look at very carefully. Governor Bailey, we're under... we want to make sure that we're not behind schedule all day, as they were yesterday. So, thank you very much. Thanks, Stephanie. Welcome back. We now turn to our next keynote, which will be livestreamed. It's a pleasure to welcome Jeffrey Schmid, President and CEO of the Federal Reserve Bank of Kansas City, and a member of the Federal Open Market Committee, where US monetary policy is set. He brings more than four decades of experience in banking and financial supervision, and today leads the Kansas City Fed's work across a large and diverse region of the United States. He is also host of the Jackson Hole Economic Policy Symposium, one of the most important global forums of central banking and economic policy. Following his remarks, we will have a Q&A moderated by Stephanie Flanders from Bloomberg. Please join me in welcoming President Jeffrey Schmid. Thank you. All right. Well, good morning, everyone. Thank you very much for that. And first of all, if you'll indulge me, I do want to make a few thank-yous. First of all, to Governor Yngveson. He's become a really good friend through the Jackson Hole Symposium. He's also become a bit of a celebrity in Jackson Hole, which is kind of fun to watch. Just a couple of quick comments because I've very much enjoyed my time here. You know, as a host of the Jackson Hole Symposium, now going on 49 years, it's amazing what the teams have to do behind the scenes in both hosting organizations, and I have a real, extreme appreciation for the teams that do this and how welcoming they are.
