The reason we built our large platform the way we have, with a lot of scale, is because that's what the entrepreneurs want, and they, you know, they, they—that expresses itself in, you know, high win rates of deals and large ownership of, of things that matter. So, you know, one of the consequences of how fast this happened, this AI wave, is the companies run into big company problems very early in their lives. And so, we need to adapt the way we build our firm. And so, you know, that's part of the reason that we've scaled up, you know, some hiring; we're building out a much broader platform that includes, that includes things like international, like channel, you know, where we, we've already got like experts in pricing and, you know, how you scale a salesforce and, and all those things, in addition to all the things that we've always done for companies. The reason is the companies are staying private longer, and the companies just need it really early in their lives. You know, like Cursor as an example is, you know, billions of dollars of revenue and, they're very small, and it's very early in their life. And the previous generation of technology didn't happen so fast, so they didn't encounter things like major business deals they had to negotiate, you know, supplier relationships that were complex, cloud deals, you know, international expansion. It's all just happening so much sooner. And so, I think part of the market share gains, if you will, that, that we've seen is just—this is entrepreneurs expressing their preferences. Yeah. Yeah, so it's funny, one of my colleagues was at a conference yesterday that was run by the, the UK Venture Capital Association, and they, they surveyed the audience saying, you know, "What do you think about AI valuations today?" You know, "Too high, about right, you know, too low?" 80% said too high, about 6% said too low. And, and as I kind of think of, of that, like, the, the AI universe, it feels like that's probably about, like, the right balance, because I think 80% of companies probably are overvalued today, because we know most of the companies aren't going to work, historically. Yeah. And there's probably going to be a small subset of those companies that are massively undervalued because they're the ones that are going to emerge as the leaders, and we'll see multiples of, of, you know, where they are, where they're being valued today. And I think from an LP perspective, I, I, I really would struggle to be in your shoes today, because having to pick those individual companies... I know you can kind of put a portfolio together, but, you know, one of the advantages I think from, from being in the LP seat is that we can have a really broad and diversified portfolio of, you know, the potential outliers in that AI space. And we know historically that that basket will increase in value over time even as the majority of those companies might fall away. Yeah. Yeah, yeah, yeah. Look, this, this dynamic is exactly why it's so important for our business to be, you know, centered around early stage. And so, we, we have to do the early-stage investments in those companies that end up working out, and then many won't work out, but that's the nature of the beast. And so, you know, our business kind of starts and ends with how successful the early-stage business is. And then at the growth stage, you know, a lot of the stuff that we spend our time trying to think about, you know, is similar to the venture stuff that I described—our lens on the venture side—but also, you know, how much do we invest in a given company in a given situation. And so, you know, slugging percentage is very well covered as an industry topic, but we really have to get slugging percentage right because of that sort of risk dynamic that you described. Yeah, yeah. We also get, you know, lots of questions around: "Are we in an AI bubble?" And, and one of the things that, it, it feels, that feels different today is, is that, you know, typically bubbles are characterized by excess supply destroying the economics. Oh, yeah, yeah. You know, today, like, we are, we're in a situation where there's, there's, there's scarcity. You know, there's not enough compute, not enough memory, not enough data centers, not enough power. It feels like we are, we are supply-constrained, not demand-constrained. And, and, you know, how, how do you think that kind of changes the, the shape of the, the sort of cycle? You know, first of all, it's, it, it's probably a healthy thing right now that we're supply-constrained, only in the sense that, you know, it probably makes it less likely that we have a bubble. Yeah. I feel pretty confident saying that we're not in a bubble right now. I'm less confident, you know, that we won't be in a bubble three years from now, but all, all I can speak to is where we are right now. We're massively supply-constrained. You can't get data center capacity at scale until late '28, early '29 right now, and that's just a fact. I think that's going to get harder. I think we're probably a year behind schedule what people would expect for data center build-out in the US, so we're already behind. We're supply-constrained in pretty much everything in the supply chain for the data center. Part of that is, you know, TSMC showing restraint and, you know, trying to be balanced, you know, but part of that is just other components that are hardware that are hard to manufacture and spin up to meet demand. And then if you look at the build-out expectations over the next four or five years, you know, if we spend 5 trillion of CapEx, you know, can you get 1 or 2 trillion dollars of revenue as a, as a return on that? The SpaceX IPO: Science Fiction or Serious Investment? | The Weekly Wrap

Steve Eisman(175.0K)·18:27·47.4K views·2026-05-29
Steve EismanHost, The Weekly Wrap Elon MuskCEO, SpaceX
TL;DRSpaceX's upcoming IPO seeks an unprecedented $1.75 to $2 trillion valuation (~100x sales) that relies on sci-fi narrative appeal and relaxed Nasdaq index rules rather than its decelerating fundamentals.
Meanwhile, traditional tech showcases a sharp split: hardware giants...

TL;DR

SpaceX's upcoming IPO seeks an unprecedented $1.75 to $2 trillion valuation (~100x sales) that relies on sci-fi narrative appeal and relaxed Nasdaq index rules rather than its decelerating fundamentals. Meanwhile, traditional tech showcases a sharp split: hardware giants like Dell are booming on real AI infrastructure spend, while software players like Salesforce face pressure as AI disruption fears linger.

The SpaceX IPO: Sci-Fi Valuation vs. Fundamental Reality

  • The Valuation Disconnect: SpaceX is aiming for a valuation of $1.75 to $2 trillion, translating to a price-to-sales multiple of roughly 100x.
      • *Peer Comparison:* By comparison, Palantir trades at under 70x trailing sales, and Nvidia trades at a price-to-revenue ratio of only 14x despite posting 85% revenue growth.
      • *Market Cap vs. Revenue:* At $2 trillion, SpaceX would be the 7th largest company by market cap, yet its revenue ranks around 200th—on par with Kellogg's.
  • Manipulated Index Inclusion: Nasdaq has adjusted its standard rules to guarantee the IPO's success. Despite SpaceX floating only about 5% of its shares (roughly $75 billion), Nasdaq will allow it to enter major indices just 15 days after the IPO. Furthermore, Nasdaq will multiply SpaceX’s float by three times for its index weight calculations.
  • Targeting Retail Investors: Elon Musk is reserving an unprecedented 30% of the IPO allocation for retail investors. Eisman argues this is because the valuation can only be sustained if SpaceX becomes a "retail cult stock."
  • Financial Breakdown (Q1 2026):
      • *Overall Results:* SpaceX generated $4.7 billion in revenue for Q1 2026, representing 15% year-over-year growth—a stark deceleration from its 33% revenue growth in 2025. The company posted a net loss of $1.9 billion for the quarter.
      • *By Division:*
          • Connectivity (Starlink): Generated $3.3 billion in revenue (up 32% year-over-year) and is the company's only profitable business unit.
          • Space (Rocket Launches): Generated $619 million, down 28% due to launch mission timing and government contract delays.
          • AI (Grok + X): Generated $818 million but remains highly unprofitable.
      • *Exaggerated TAM:* The IPO prospectus claims a total addressable market (TAM) of $28.5 trillion. Strikingly, 93% ($26.5 trillion) of this TAM is attributed to its AI division, which is currently its smallest, most unprofitable unit and is reportedly bypassed by SpaceX's own engineers for being subpar.

Tech Earnings: Dell's Hardware Boom vs. Salesforce's Software Struggles

  • Dell Technologies' AI Capex Tailwind: Dell's stock has surged 148% year-to-date, demonstrating how heavily AI infrastructure spending is benefiting the hardware sector.
      • *Q1 Performance:* Dell reported powerful earnings with revenue up 39% and EPS up 45%, prompting management to raise guidance for both the next quarter and the full fiscal year.
  • Salesforce's Mixed Results: Salesforce beat expectations on its top and bottom lines for the quarter, but issued soft guidance that triggered negative market sentiment.
      • *The Guidance Miss:* Q2 revenue guidance of $11.27 to $11.35 billion missed Wall Street expectations of $11.36 billion. This guidance miss plays into broader market anxieties that generative AI represents a structural threat to traditional SaaS business models.
  • Critique of Stock-Based Compensation: Eisman noted that while Salesforce reported an adjusted EPS of $3.88, its GAAP EPS was a much lower $2.42 due to heavy stock-based compensation. He criticized the software industry's standard practice of excluding stock-based compensation from adjusted metrics, calling it "ridiculous."

Financial Sector Outlook: Bank of America & Credit Ratings Oligopolies

  • Bank of America (BAC) Bull Case: Eisman defended BAC against recent high-profile hedge fund exits.
      • *NIM Recovery:* BAC hurt its Net Interest Margin (NIM) during the pandemic by buying low-yield, long-dated Treasuries. However, because those bonds are now rolling off, BAC's NIM is poised to improve regardless of Federal Reserve policy.
      • *Strong ROE:* BAC boasts a 16% return on tangible common equity. With no U.S. recession on the immediate horizon to drive up credit losses, Eisman views the stock as a solid holding.
  • The Ratings Agencies (Moody's and S&P Global): Eisman remains highly bullish on both ratings giants, owning Moody's himself.
      • *Explaining the 5-Year Underperformance:* Both stocks lagged the broader market over the last five years because the zero-rate environment of 2020–2021 pulled a decade of corporate debt refinancing forward. This created an issuance drought from 2022 to 2024, which is finally ending as debt issuance picks back up.
      • *The Ethical Conundrum:* Addressing investor concerns over the agencies' role in stamping AAA ratings on toxic subprime debt prior to the 2008 Great Financial Crisis, Eisman noted that while they "sold their souls" for fees back then, both companies are now under entirely different management teams.

Notable Quotes

"At a valuation of 1.75 to 2 trillion, SpaceX will have the seventh largest market cap. But in terms of revenue, it ranks around 200 with Kellogg's. Of course, selling Froot Loops is a mundane but profitable business." — Steve Eisman
"Personally, I think excluding stock-based comp from earnings is ridiculous." — Steve Eisman

Even though SpaceX is only going to float around 5% of its shares, Nasdaq decided that, given the potential $1.75 to $2 trillion total valuation, a float that equates to around 75 billion needs to join the index. Now, normally, Nasdaq would not allow a 5% floating stock to enter into any indices, or at least not for many, many, many months. Also, the index weighting would be small. SpaceX, however, will be allowed to enter indices only 15 days after the IPO. Also, instead of getting a small weighting, Nasdaq will multiply SpaceX's float by three times for index calculations. Finally, Musk is reserving 30% of the offering for retail. This is unheard of for a serious company. In the usual IPO, shares are mostly allocated to institutions, and retail receives a pittance. So why do it this way? Because the valuation is kind of crazy, and the only way to justify it is for SpaceX to become a retail cult stock. SpaceX is composed of three divisions: the first, Space, this is the rocket launching business for satellites; two, Connectivity, this is Starlink, which provides mobile connectivity all over the globe; and three, AI, a combination of AI data centers and an LLM called Grok plus X. The word "grok" means to understand something deeply and profoundly. Clearly, Elon Musk read this book and takes his science fiction very seriously. By the way, I read that novel when I was in high school. If the volume of global debt has grown so dramatically over the last several years, the ratings agencies should have seen a corresponding structural lift in their earnings and stock prices. They haven't—not over the last 5 years.'" "S&P's 5-year total return is roughly 24% against a broader market that approximately doubled. The explanation I've pieced together is that 2020 and '21 were extraordinary issuance years driven by near-zero rates. Companies essentially pulled a decade of refinancing forward into 2 years, leaving an issuance drought from 2022 through 2024. At the same time, both stocks experienced significant multiple compression." "The ethical question I'm generally torn on, and I'll be direct about something that gives me pause: investing in Moody's or S&P requires me to get comfortable with their role in 2008. I know they stamped AAA on instruments that deserved junk status. What I don't fully understand is the allocation of responsibility. Were the agencies the primary driver or were they captured by the investment banks structuring the products?" "I'm not looking for absolution, but I think there is a meaningful difference between a structurally captured institution and a predatory one. If you have a view on where the agencies actually sat in that chain of culpability, it would genuinely help me decide." There's a lot here, so let's take them one at a time. First, with respect to the banks in general, and Bank of America in particular. It's true that they are capital-intensive businesses and the regulators restrict their leverage. That has always been true. Bank of America's return on tangible common equity is actually 16%, which is quite good given how much the banks have been forced to delever. It's also true that banks never do well in a recession because credit costs climb. However, for now, I just don't see a recession on the horizon. With respect to the company's net interest margin, Bank of America made a mistake during COVID and buried itself in long-dated Treasuries. That move has hurt its NIM, but those bonds are rolling off, so the net interest margin should improve over the next several years, regardless of Fed policy. I think it's okay to own Bank of America. With respect to the ratings agencies, I like both of them because they are oligopolies. The stocks have underperformed because debt issuance exploded during COVID as rates went to zero. Issuance has been light for the last several years, but that is now coming to an end, and issuance is picking up. I like both companies, and I own Moody's myself. As for your ethical conundrum, it's true that the ratings agencies did bad things leading up to the GFC. They were paid three to four times more to rate subprime ABS and CDOs, so they sold their souls to make the extra fees. However, today they are owned by different management teams, so I think you don't have to agonize over owning these stocks. This last Monday, May 25th, we posted an interview with Peter Arment, the defense and aerospace analyst at Baird. We mostly focused on defense companies and which ones are best positioned. Peter shared an amazing story of how the government forced the defense companies to merge in the 1990s. They became too big to pivot, and smaller, more nimble companies are taking share. Given the war in Iran and the continuing war in Ukraine, questions about which stocks to own, if any, keep appearing. Peter's coverage could not be more relevant, so check it out. Treasury Secretary Scott Bessent: Economic Security Is National Security | Keynote Remarks at RNEF

Ronald Reagan Presidential Foundation & Institute(1.2M)·44:47·345 views·2026-05-29
Scott BessentU.S. Treasury Secretary
TL;DRTreasury Secretary Scott Bessent outlines the Trump administration's "economic security is national security" doctrine, detailing the devastating impacts of the "Operation Economic Fury" financial blockade on Iran, alongside domestic policies aimed…
at reshoring critical...

TL;DR

Treasury Secretary Scott Bessent outlines the Trump administration's "economic security is national security" doctrine, detailing the devastating impacts of the "Operation Economic Fury" financial blockade on Iran, alongside domestic policies aimed at reshoring critical supply chains, implementing permanent tax cuts, and establishing Trump Savings Accounts for American children.

The "Economic Security is National Security" Doctrine

  • Shift from Consumption to Production: Bessent argues that for decades, America mistakenly prioritized cheap consumer imports over manufacturing resilience. He calls for a structural return to measuring economic health at the "factory gate" rather than the "checkout counter."
  • The True Cost of Offshoring:
      • Allowing key factories in states like Michigan, Ohio, and Pennsylvania to go dark degraded America's "industrial commons"—the ecosystem of skilled labor, tooling, and local logistics needed for rapid adaptation.
      • The COVID-19 pandemic and the war in Ukraine exposed the danger of "just-in-time" supply chains over "just-in-case" preparedness, revealing severe vulnerabilities in defense surge capacity, medical supplies, and advanced technologies.
  • Separating Trade from Geopolitics: Past administrations treated commercial trade and national security as distinct silos. Bessent asserts that relying on strategic rivals like China for critical inputs inevitably collapses these silos, endangering national sovereignty.

"Operation Economic Fury" and the Blockade of Iran

  • A Multi-Front Economic Blockade: In coordination with the U.S. Navy, the Treasury has instituted a severe financial and maritime blockade. The Navy has shut down Iran’s primary oil export facility on Kharg Island, reducing Iranian oil-on-the-water to historical lows.
  • Severe Domestic Crises in Iran: According to Bessent, "Operation Economic Fury" has brought Iran to the end of its financial tether:
      • Inflation in Iran has spiked past 200%, forcing the regime to issue food vouchers and shut down the internet.
      • Approximately 40% to 50% of Iranian military and police forces are currently unpaid or failing to report to work.
  • Asset and Cryptocurrency Seizures:
      • The Treasury has frozen extensive offshore accounts belonging to the Islamic Revolutionary Guard Corps (IRGC), which Bessent claims was embezzling $400 million to $500 million per month from the Iranian people.
      • The U.S. government has outright seized approximately $1 billion in IRGC-linked cryptocurrency wallets.
  • Diplomatic Red Lines: Bessent confirms that while Iran has engaged in negotiations for the first time, President Trump’s core "red lines" remain firm: full denuclearization, no uranium enrichment, free and open access to the Strait of Hormuz, and no short-term financial relief.

Trade Policy & Rebuilding Critical Industrial Capacity

  • America First Trade Memorandum: Signed on President Trump's first day back in office, this directive instructs the Treasury, Commerce, Homeland Security, and USTR to treat trade as an instrument of national strategy rather than a technocratic afterthought.
  • Strategic Tariffs and Section 232 Actions:
      • The administration declared a national emergency regarding foreign trade practices, implementing reciprocal tariff actions explicitly built around national security.
      • Section 232 executive actions were signed to limit foreign import dependencies on processed critical minerals and derivative products.
  • Maritime and Shipbuilding Dominance: To combat America's shrunken shipbuilding capacity, the administration established a new Office of Maritime Industrial Capacity at the National Security Council to restore domestic shipping and logistics resilience.
  • Pharmaceutical Sovereignty: President Trump signed an executive order to fill the strategic Active Pharmaceutical Ingredients (API) reserve, introducing parallel Section 232 investigations and tariffs on foreign patented pharmaceuticals to secure domestic medicine supplies.

Domestic Economic Strategy: Tax Permanence, Trump Accounts, and the Fed

  • Permanence in Tax Policy: Bessent highlights the "One Big Beautiful Bill," which permanently codifies the tax cuts and full business expensing from the Trump 1.0 Tax Cuts and Jobs Act, providing long-term market certainty.
  • The Trump Savings Accounts Initiative: Described by Bessent as the most significant government benefit for young people since the GI Bill, the initiative aims to build a new class of American shareholders:
      • Seed Funding: Every child born during President Trump's term receives a $1,000 seed investment from the Treasury, placed into low-cost index funds.
      • Tax-Free Growth: Families can contribute up to $5,000 annually, compounding tax-free until age 18 to be used for education, starting a business, or a home down payment.
      • Private Philanthropy: Philanthropists Michael and Susan Dell have pledged $6.25 billion directly to the program to fund accounts for children residing in the bottom 80% of economic ZIP codes.
  • A "New Day" at the Federal Reserve: Bessent praises newly confirmed Fed Chairman Kevin Warsh. He anticipates a return to central banking basics, highlighting Warsh's intent to eliminate "forward guidance" in favor of market-driven credibility and Fed accountability.
  • Strong Dollar Policy: Bessent clarifies that a strong dollar is not defined by daily screen prices, but by structural tax, trade, and energy policies that make the U.S. the safest and highest-yielding destination for global capital.

Notable Quotes

"Economic security is national security. For a nation that cannot manufacture, mine, ship, or refine its needs gradually cedes its strength and sovereignty to others." — Scott Bessent, U.S. Treasury Secretary
"In a crisis, the nation with a broad and skilled industrial base has options; the nation that has hollowed out that base only has excuses." — Scott Bessent, U.S. Treasury Secretary
"While America slept, our vulnerabilities grew, but under President Trump's leadership, we are alert to the risk we can no longer ignore and attuned to the responsibility we can no longer defer." — Scott Bessent, U.S. Treasury Secretary
Thank you very much. It's an honor to be here, and I'd like to thank the Ronald Reagan Presidential Foundation and Institute for inviting me to join you today. Of course, this year's forum carries special significance as we celebrate 250 years of the American story. But milestones of this magnitude demand more than ceremony. They ask something of us. They invite us to reflect not just on the creation of our country, but on its condition. And as we focus today on America's economic future, they compel us to confront and correct decisions that have diminished our sovereignty in recent decades. President Reagan himself understood that renewal begins with reflection. "One thing that has made our Republic great," he said, "is that we don't hide from our mistakes, we learn from them, then we go on and do things better than we did before, better than we did before." That is the spirit in which I want to speak with you today, with candor and with confidence in our capacity to deliver another 250 years of American economic leadership. The truth is that for too long, America has been asleep. We mistook comfort for strength, we substituted efficiency for resilience, and we used consumption as a measure of prosperity. We told ourselves that so long as goods were cheaper overseas, it did not matter whether factories went dark in Michigan, Ohio, or Pennsylvania. We assumed that supply chains would always function smoothly, that adversaries would always behave responsibly, and that the invisible hand would correct vulnerabilities that too few in public life had the courage to confront. And while we reassured ourselves with those assumptions, risk accumulated all around us. Somewhere along the way, we lost sight of a foundational principle that previous generations understood instinctively: economic security is national security. For a nation that cannot manufacture, mine, ship, or refine its needs gradually cedes its strength and sovereignty to others. That is a dangerous dependency for any country. It is an unacceptable one for the United States of America. Now, nations rarely cede their security in a single moment. More often, they drift into dependence through a series of politically expedient decisions. For our part, we made a series of mistakes—some bipartisan, others ideological, and many defended long after their cost became impossible to ignore. One was treating trade policy as though it existed apart from national strategy. We convinced ourselves that we could pursue commercial partnerships in one silo while managing security relationships in another, as if the world would honor these distinctions. But a nation that relies on a rival for critical inputs, finances the rise of countries that do not share our interests, and allows its productive base to erode while promising to defend the international order will find, sooner or later, that these two silos collapse into each other. It was as damaging as our willingness to extend strategic trust where it had been... not earned. China's accession to the WTO and the granting of permanent normal trade relations were sold to the American people as steps that would level markets and moderate behavior. Instead, we left our workers to compete against state-led subsidies, excess capacity, and practices that distort trade and undermine reciprocity. We assumed the rules-based system would discipline these behaviors, only to find that in too many sectors, it accommodated them. And in the name of efficiency, we began to celebrate "just in time" while neglecting "just in case". The pandemic did not create our brittle supply chains so much as expose them. Suddenly, the world's most advanced economy found itself partially dependent on foreign suppliers for semiconductors, large-capacity batteries, critical minerals, and medicines that we no longer produced here at home. Regrettably, our recovery from the pandemic did not bring reform under the prior administration. Today, the United States still imports most of its rare earth minerals that are essential to the technologies that will shape military and economic power in the 21st century. Only a small share of the active pharmaceutical ingredients that we use in the United States are made here. And American shipbuilding capacity, which once helped to secure victory in war and prosperity in peace, has shrunk to a fraction of global production. So when I say America has been asleep, I do not mean that no one warned us. In fact, many did. Workers warned us when their plants shuttered and communities decayed. Military planners warned us when supply chains narrowed. Manufacturers warned us about subsidized competitors and dumped products. Public health officials warned us about pharmaceutical dependencies. The warning lights were glaring all around us. But our political class preferred the comfort of old formulas: cheaper was always better, offshoring was inevitable, industrial policy was unfashionable, and strategic dependence was acceptable so long as the cost remained invisible. Beneath every one of those mistakes lay a more basic failure in our philosophy: in reducing economics to consumption, we forgot production. We measured abundance at the checkout counter rather than at the factory gate. We talked about GDP but not enough about its composition, and we prized low-cost inputs without first asking whether a nation can remain sovereign when it loses command over the things that matter most. A country cannot outsource its industrial commons, ignore strategic concentration, and expect to remain secure. For manufacturing is more than output on a balance sheet; it is a reservoir of practical capability: engineers and welders, tool and die makers, logistic networks, plant managers, and workers who know how to solve problems on the factory floor. When the ecosystem is strong, a country can adapt quickly. When it is hollowed out, adaptation becomes slower, more costly, and less certain. We saw this during COVID in the scramble for ventilators, masks, and other medical equipment. We saw it again as the war in Ukraine reminded the world that high-intensity conflict consumes weapons and munitions at a pace far beyond peacetime assumptions. Surge capacity cannot be conjured overnight; it must be built before a crisis arrives. And a country that allows its industrial base to shrink in the quiet years should not be surprised when it struggles to replenish stocks in the danger ones. In a crisis, the nation with a broad and skilled industrial base has options; the nation that has hollowed out that base only has excuses. America should never again put itself in a position where its security depends on supply chains it does not control, factories it no longer maintains, or skilled trades it failed to preserve. That is why the President's agenda matters. Reasonable people can debate the calibration of any particular instrument, but the central strategic insight is undoubtedly sound: trade policy, industrial capacity, and national security are inseparable. And to allow foreign dependencies to degrade any one of these domains is to allow them to define America's future. So, on his first day back in office, President Trump issued the America First Trade Policy memorandum, a sweeping directive to Treasury, Commerce, Homeland Security, USTR, and other agencies to stop treating trade as a technocratic afterthought instead of an instrument of national strategy. Last April, the President declared a national emergency tied to the cumulative effects of foreign trade practices and announced reciprocal tariff actions, framing them explicitly around national and economic security. Again, people can debate design, sequencing, and scope. Healthy democracies should debate those questions. But no one should miss the larger point: chronic, non-reciprocal agreements, industrial hollowing, and strategic import dependence are not questions of commerce alone; they are fissures in our country's foundation. A few weeks later, President Trump signed executive action on processed critical minerals and derivative products under Section 232, expressly linking import dependence to risk for national security, defense readiness, price stability, and economic resilience. Some have tried to weaponize critical minerals against us, but this administration is ensuring they will not succeed. On maritime strength, President Trump signed an executive order to restore America's maritime dominance, established a new Office of Maritime Industrial Capacity at the National Security Council, and called for a maritime action plan to rebuild US shipbuilding capabilities. This is what it looks like to connect industrial capacity, logistics resilience, workforce development, and national security in one strategic frame. Finally, on pharmaceuticals, President Trump signed an executive order to fill the strategic active pharmaceutical ingredients reserve so that America is never again caught flat-footed when access to critical drug components is at risk. Last month, the White House also highlighted further action to impose tariffs on patented pharmaceutical products while noting parallel Section 232 investigations involving personal protective equipment, medical consumable devices, and robotics. The strategic message is unmistakable: supply chain resilience in medicine will no longer be an afterthought. This broader reorientation is also visible in the administration's trade agenda for 2026, as we work to secure reciprocity, strengthen domestic production, and align America's external economic relationships with our core national interest. Taken together, these efforts reflect the broader principle that President Trump has articulated clearly: economic security is national security. But let me be direct about what that doctrine does not mean. It does not mean retreating from the world. On the contrary, it means engaging with it on stronger, fairer, and more sustainable terms. It does not mean severing ties indiscriminately. It means distinguishing healthy interdependence from dangerous overdependence. It does not mean rejecting efficiency. It means refusing to worship efficiency when efficiency leaves our nation exposed. And it does not mean treating allies and adversaries alike. It means bringing our trading relationships and our security relationships back into alignment by building supply chains with trusted partners where appropriate, rebuilding capacity at home where necessary, and ensuring that the economic architecture supporting American power strengthens American security. Of course, America still holds enormous advantages, among them the world's deepest capital markets, extraordinary innovators, abundant energy potential, leading universities, an unmatched entrepreneurial culture, and workers who can outbuild and outcompete anyone so long as the playing field is fair. All we needed was a government willing to declare that decline is a choice, and renewal can be one too. So yes, while America slept, our vulnerabilities grew. While America slept, too many leaders accepted fragile supply chains as the price of modern life. While America slept, we let industrial policy erode and Main Street decay. We tolerated unfairness in the name of stability and conflated market access with strategic success. But America has awakened. We are alert to the fact that a resilient economy is the foundation of a strong Republic, that we measure the health of our economy not merely by what it produces but whom it lifts, that secure supply chains matter as much as stock indices, that productive capacity is power, and that trade policy, industrial policy, and national security policy must all fit together or they will each fail separately. So let me end where I began: with President Reagan's reminder to identify where we went wrong and to recover our faith in what can go right. If we learn from our mistakes, if we rebuild what we neglected, if we reward production as well as consumption, if we prize resilience alongside prosperity, as President Trump has been doing, then this country will not simply patch over our vulnerabilities. It will enter a new season of national strength with a more robust Main Street and industrial base than ever before. We will see safer supply chains, more secure medicines, more reliable access to critical minerals, a revived maritime capacity, a financial system aligned with a national purpose, and trading partnerships that reinforce rather than undermine our security relationships. Indeed, the strength of this Republic lies in not denying that the old consensus failed. Instead, we must... we must restore resilience where complacency took hold, rebuild capacity where dependence took root, and above all, recover the confidence to believe a nation that knows how to build, invent, produce, and lead is never destined for decline. While America slept, our vulnerabilities grew, but under President Trump's leadership, we are alert to the risks we can no longer ignore and attuned to the responsibility we can no longer defer. Under President Trump, we have awoken to morning again in America, and our best days, I believe, still lie ahead. Thank you all, I look forward to exploring these themes in further conversation with Larry. Thank you. Thank you. Thank you, everybody. Thank you, Mr. Secretary, for an excellent speech. Trump deal with Iran, gas prices, economics & more addressed during Scott Bessent, Larry Kudlow chat

USA TODAY(7.5M)·28:12·24.8K views·2026-05-29
Scott BessentU.S. Treasury Secretary Larry KudlowHost, Fox Business
TL;DRTreasury Secretary Scott Bessent outlines how the administration's "Operation Economic Fury" and naval blockade have financially crippled Iran, forcing them to negotiate on nuclear enrichment, while domestic supply-side economic reforms and the newly launched Trump Savings Accounts continue to drive historic U.S.

TL;DR

Treasury Secretary Scott Bessent outlines how the administration's "Operation Economic Fury" and naval blockade have financially crippled Iran, forcing them to negotiate on nuclear enrichment, while domestic supply-side economic reforms and the newly launched Trump Savings Accounts continue to drive historic U.S. business investment and consumer resilience.

***

The Economic Blockade and Negotiations with Iran

  • Economic Stragulation: Through "Operation Economic Fury," the Treasury Department and the U.S. Navy have successfully shut down Iran’s financial pipelines, leaving the nation at the end of its financial tether.
      • Internal Collapse: Iran is experiencing over 200% inflation, is distributing food vouchers, has cut off internet access, and is failing to pay 40% to 50% of its military and police forces.
      • Decapitated Leadership: Diplomatic efforts are dealing with "third-level" officials, as the first two tiers of leadership have been decapitated. Negotiations are complicated by navigating both a cleric-led theocracy and an IRGC-led "thugocracy."
  • Naval Blockade and Oil Shutoff: The U.S. Navy has successfully blockaded Iranian ports, including Kharg Island, Iran's primary oil export facility, completely shutting it down. Iran's only remaining oil client is China, and their volume of oil on the water is at its lowest historical levels.
  • Seizure of Assets:
      • Crypto and Offshore Accounts: Treasury has directly seized approximately $1 billion in cryptocurrency wallets belonging to Iranian actors and is working with European allies to freeze luxury villas and properties.
      • IRGC Theft: Prior to the blockade, leaders of the Islamic Revolutionary Guard Corps (IRGC) were estimated to be stealing $400 million to $500 million per month, divided among roughly 80 leaders.
      • GCC Alignment: Following missile and drone attacks from Iran, GCC Gulf allies have closed banking loopholes and partnered with the U.S. to freeze illicit Iranian accounts.

U.S. Economic Boom and Supply-Side Success

  • Historic Business Investment: Despite temporary energy price spikes, non-defense capital goods and business investment have grown at an annualized rate of nearly 19% over the past three months (10.5% year-over-year).
      • Productivity & Unit Labor Costs: Productivity is up nearly 3%, while unit labor costs—a critical long-term inflation metric—sit at a low 1.2% over the last four quarters.
  • The "One Big Beautiful Bill": Bessent credited permanent tax cuts, full expensing, and the collaboration of House Speaker Mike Johnson, Jason Smith, French Hill, Jodey Arrington, and Leader John Thune for establishing permanent policy certainty for U.S. businesses.
  • Consumer Purchasing Power: While gasoline prices reached a temporary high of $4.50 before beginning to decline, the average household impact was limited to under $200 in extra fuel costs.
      • Grocery Price Correction: Wholesale food prices are stabilizing. While beef remains high due to cattle disease in Mexico, egg prices are down 90%, and chicken and vegetables are also down.

Trump Savings Accounts and the 250th American Anniversary

  • Trump Savings Accounts (TSAs): Hailed by Bessent as the most significant government benefit for young people since the GI Bill, TSAs aim to expand equity market exposure to the 38% of Americans who currently have none.
      • Funding Structure: Children born during the President's term will receive a $1,000 seed investment from the Treasury into low-cost index funds.
      • Family Contributions: Families can contribute up to $5,000 tax-free annually until the child reaches age 18. Funds can then be rolled into retirement plans, used for higher education, or utilized as a down payment on a home.
      • Philanthropic Support: Billionaire philanthropists Michael and Susan Dell have committed $6.25 billion directly to TSAs for children in the bottom 80% of economic zip codes. Additionally, 20 states have committed to topping up these accounts.
  • Proposed $250 Currency Note: In celebration of America's 250th anniversary, there is active legislative interest in issuing a $250 bill featuring President Trump, modeled after the Calvin Coolidge coin minted for the 150th anniversary.
      • Seigniorage Advantage: Bessent noted that printing a $250 bill acts as an interest-free loan to the government because citizens are highly likely to hold and collect the physical currency rather than spend it.

Monetary Policy and the "Warsh Fed"

  • A New Era at the Federal Reserve: Bessent expressed high confidence in newly confirmed Fed Chairman Kevin Warsh, predicting a return to institutional credibility, transparency, and simplicity.
      • Forward Guidance: Bessent confirmed 100% agreement with Chairman Warsh's objective to eliminate "forward guidance" in favor of returning to standard Fed accountability.
  • Fed Independence: Bessent reiterated that President Trump intends to let the Fed Chairman operate independently without political pressure regarding interest rate adjustments.
  • Strong Dollar Policy: Bessent, a 35-year currency market veteran, affirmed that the administration remains fully committed to maintaining the U.S. dollar as the global reserve currency, defined by maintaining reliable, long-term real returns for global investors.

***

So you just had breakfast with our new Fed Chairman, Kevin Warsh. How was that? It was great. It was, you know, as President Reagan would say, it's a new day at the Fed. So we now have the Warsh Fed. The Treasury Secretary and the Fed chair have lunch or breakfast every week. It's a home game and an away game, so I did the away game at the Fed. Their food's much better because they don't have a budget—they print their own money! Apparently not, one hears. But it was good. Renewal and change is good, and I think we are going to see a new sheriff in town. I'm not going to get out ahead of the Chairman, but he said he's going to get rid of forward guidance. You approve of that? One hundred percent. One hundred percent. And I also think we're going to get back to basics in terms of accountability, credibility, and what the purpose of the Fed is. Because Larry, your career is longer than mine, but for a long time, the Fed just ran in the background. It wasn't, "Oh, it's Fed Day, it's the Fed meeting, cover the press conference." It wasn't an all-day event with all these governors out giving speeches and popping off. I think you are going to see much more focus and a lot more credibility. At the end of the day, financial markets are all about credibility. I noticed the President said right at the beginning, as soon as Kevin was confirmed, that he's on his own. There's really no pressure to move interest rates in one way or another, and that he's just going to trust his Fed Chairman. I just wanted to be sure that the Treasury Secretary echoed those views. One hundred percent. The President made the decision, I ran the process, and the President made the decision. I never put my thumb on the scale for Kevin Warsh, whom I've known for 20 years. I think he's an incredible choice, and the President got there for all the right reasons. The President understands that the more credible you are, the more work the financial markets will do for you. So there is no pressure at the moment. Look, again, a lot of the publications wanted to be alarmist, but to the extent I had any success in my 35-year Wall Street career, it was usually doing the opposite of what was on the front page. And when I saw over the past couple of weeks, "What a terrible time to start up as the Fed chair," I thought, no, it's a great time. It's a great time. Interest rates have spiked, and inflation has spiked. The tough thing would have been to come in in February when the market was pricing in two or three rate cuts for the year, inflation looked like it was coming back down to target, and growth was high. It is much better to come in now. And look, you all can be the judge. I'm not saying whether it's causation or correlation, but rates peaked the day before Chair Warsh was sworn in. And also tell me if this is right: I think I'm noticing during this Iranian war episode, with all of the good things that are going on, the dollar is actually improving. If I'm not mistaken, you yourself have been a strong advocate of maintaining the dollar as a reserve currency. I know in the first term when I served, the President was always a strong advocate to maintain the dollar as a reserve currency. Has any of that changed? Nothing's changed. And again, I was in the currency market for 35 years, and I think people have the wrong notion of what a strong dollar means. A strong dollar isn't just the price on the screen every second, every minute, every day, or every week. What a strong dollar means is that we are doing the right things to set our economy on a good course, so that people will want to hold dollars, feel safe and secure, and know their money will give back to them high real returns. And you know, I think we've done that, whether it's through trade policy, tax policy, or energy policy. The United States—President Trump with his policies—has made the United States the best place. Whether you want to be in the liquid markets or come build your factory, you will have great certainty. You will have great certainty on tax, on energy, and on our workforce. Secretary Scott Bessent, good friend, thank you for your service and thank you for your good works for America. We appreciate it very much. Good. Well, thank you, Larry.

TL;DR

Treasury Secretary Scott Bessent outlined a highly resilient US economic outlook, clarified that any tentative 60-day ceasefire deal with Iran is contingent on strict nuclear and maritime red lines, and previewed the July 4th launch of "Trump accounts" designed to build a new generation of American shareholders.

Iran Negotiations and Energy Security

  • Tentative 60-Day Ceasefire: Secretary Bessent confirmed that while negotiations are ongoing, any deal is entirely dependent on President Trump’s explicit red lines.
      • Core Conditions: Iran must surrender all highly enriched uranium, permanently dismantle its nuclear program, and guarantee free, open navigation through the Strait of Hormuz.
      • Sanctions Relief: Bessent emphasized that sanctions relief remains frozen and will not be granted until concrete, verifiable progress is made on these core demands.
  • Strait of Hormuz and Oil Supply: Despite temporary maritime disruptions, global oil markets remain well-supplied, with oil prices down approximately 10% in May.
      • Post-Conflict Outlook: Roughly 2,000 ships are currently waiting to exit the Gulf; a rapid decline in gasoline prices is anticipated once the strait fully reopens.
      • Oman Toll Rumors: Bessent refuted concerns of conflict with Oman, stating he received assurances from the Omani ambassador that there are no plans to toll the strait.
  • Airlines Sanctions: The US plans to aggressively shut down state-owned Iranian airlines by secondary-sanctioning any entities providing refueling, ticket sales, or landing services, except for approved humanitarian or religious (Mecca/Medina) travel.

Economic Indicators and "Trump Accounts"

  • Sustained Economic Growth: Bessent highlighted a resilient US economy, citing a 2.6% real GDP increase over the past four quarters and an Atlanta Fed GDPNow estimate of 3.8% for the current quarter. Unemployment remains steady at 4.3%.
  • Trump Accounts Initiative: Launching on July 4th (via `trumpaccount.gov`), these accounts aim to expand equity exposure to the 38% of Americans currently lacking stock market access.
      • Treasury Seed Funding: The US Treasury will provide a $1,000 seed investment for every child born during the Trump administration.
      • Financial Literacy: The platform features six online "learning pods" to educate working-class families on capital markets.
  • Fiscal and Tax Performance: Average tax refunds reached nearly $3,300 this season (an 11% year-over-year increase).
      • Tax Relief: 62 million returns (44%) claimed at least one of the administration's tax cuts, such as tax-free tips, tax-free overtime, reduced taxes on Social Security, or auto loan deductibility.
      • Deficit Reduction: The administration reduced the inherited budget deficit from 6.7% to approximately 5.4% of GDP, with a long-term goal of hitting 3%. Bessent plans to target an estimated $500 billion in annual government fraud to accelerate this timeline.
  • Monetary Policy Collaboration: Bessent noted he held his first weekly breakfast with the newly appointed Fed Chair, Kevin Warsh, expressing strong confidence in a balanced approach to managing inflation and growth.

Geopolitical Sanctions and National Security

  • Maximum Pressure on Russia: Bessent defended the administration's record, stating it has executed the toughest Russian oil sanctions in history, specifically targeting LUKOIL and Rosneft.
  • Cuba Strategy: Treasury is working with Secretary of State Marco Rubio on a "carrot and stick" policy. Sanctions will remain strict unless the Cuban government permits direct humanitarian aid without routing it through corrupt state systems.
  • Antifa Funding Investigation: Ongoing Treasury and FBI investigations into the funding of Antifa have made "substantial progress."
      • IRS Oversight: The IRS is issuing new Form 990 guidance requiring non-profits to know their grant recipients to ensure funds do not support violent or rights-suppressing organizations.
  • AI Leadership & Regulation: The US is coordinating closely with top private labs to protect critical infrastructure from advanced AI models while ensuring the US maintains its significant lead over China.

Domestic Controversies and the $250 Bill

  • Proposed $250 Trump Bill: Congress is evaluating bipartisan legislation to lift the restriction against living people appearing on US currency, specifically to place Donald J. Trump on a special $250 bill for the nation's 250th anniversary. Treasury has preemptively drafted design templates to ensure legal compliance if the bill passes.
  • IRS Settlement and DOJ Transfer: Bessent declined to discuss details of the IRS settlement rolled over into the DOJ's "weaponization fund" due to active litigation. He reiterated that the dispute arose from an IRS bad actor leaking over 400,000 private tax returns belonging to the Trump family and employees.

Why Two IIT Engineers Turned Down $550K Jobs To Build A Startup

Y Combinator(2.3M)·24:30·9.8K views·2026-05-29
Varun VummadiCo-founder, Giga Ankit GuptaGeneral Partner, Y Combinator
TL;DRVarun Vummadi turned down a $550,000 quant finance offer and a Stanford PhD to build GigaML, eventually leveraging Y Combinator's network and a lean 8-person team to secure major enterprise clients like DoorDash over well-funded, 400-person competitors.
Rejecting Elite...

TL;DR

Varun Vummadi turned down a $550,000 quant finance offer and a Stanford PhD to build GigaML, eventually leveraging Y Combinator's network and a lean 8-person team to secure major enterprise clients like DoorDash over well-funded, 400-person competitors.

The Non-Linear Path to Product-Market Fit

  • Rejecting Elite Safety Nets: Driven by the launch of ChatGPT, Varun Vummadi and his co-founder turned down highly lucrative opportunities—including a $550,000 New York City quant trading offer and a Stanford PhD program—just three days before they were set to begin.
  • The YC Pivots:
      • The founders originally entered Y Combinator with an LLM-based ed-tech idea.
      • During the interview, YC Partner Harj Taggar told them the ed-tech market was unviable but accepted them anyway due to their strong engineering backgrounds.
      • After briefly pivoting to an LLM fine-tuning and caching service (which topped Hugging Face benchmarks and raised a $4 million seed round), they noticed their only growing customer use cases were coding and customer support.
      • This insight led them to pivot fully into AI-driven customer support, signing Zepto as their first major customer.

Defeating Giants: How a Lean Team Won DoorDash

  • Beating a 400-Person Competitor: Operating as a lean team of just eight people, GigaML went head-to-head with a massive, heavily funded incumbent competitor to win DoorDash's enterprise customer service contract.
  • The YC Network Multiplier: To get his foot in the door, YC President Garry Tan introduced Varun directly to DoorDash CEO Tony Xu. Because both startups were YC alumni, an inherent layer of trust allowed GigaML to bypass traditional enterprise sales friction and secure a three-month pilot.
  • Superior Deflection Metrics:
      • Traditional customer support IVR and chatbots yield a 10% to 15% deflection rate.
      • GigaML's conversational AI agents achieve a 60% to 70% deflection rate, with target goals of 90% to 95% for top-tier enterprise clients.
      • The core of their technical edge relies on "iterating markdown policy files" to consistently move customer KPIs like CSAT and resolution rates.

Driving Hyper-Leverage via Internal AI Automation

  • A 7x Engineering Force Multiplier: GigaML keeps its internal headcount incredibly low by heavily utilizing coding agents. Varun estimates that without tools like Claude, GigaML would require six to seven times more engineers to maintain their current shipping velocity.
  • "Vibe Coding" Interview Tactics: GigaML tests job candidates by allowing them to "vibe code" alongside AI assistants during the interview. To ensure deep technical understanding, they then strip away the AI tools and ask candidates to manually debug and modify the generated codebase.
  • Enterprise AI Deployments: Varun identifies the traditional "forward deployed engineer" as the primary bottleneck in enterprise AI adoption. GigaML is currently building an autonomous "AI forward deployed engineer" designed to join Slack channels and Google Meets, synthesize notes, and automatically deploy system changes.

Hard-Earned Advice for College Founders

  • Burn the Boats: Varun attributes their survival to the psychological pressure of rejecting their high-paying job offers. Having no fallback plan forced them to make the business work.
  • Charge Customers Early: Startups should avoid offering free pilots for too long. If a business problem is painful enough, a customer will pay real money or time for it. If they refuse to pay, the founder is solving a "fake problem."
  • Product Over Sales: In the generative AI era, product quality dictates success. Top AI companies like OpenAI and Anthropic scale rapidly due to value-delivery, not aggressive sales commission structures.

Notable Quotes

"We're like eight people going against this 400-person well-funded company and we won... That's when we truly realized that there is a lot of arbitrage of actually building a great product rather than a sales team." — Varun Vummadi
"It actually forces you to make things... Just like going at it and doing things has a lot stronger value. Especially with AI, because the cost of building things is so low, people should just build things and try to deliver as much value as they can to a very small set of customers and see if they can pay them money." — Varun Vummadi
"If the problem is important enough, people should be willing to pay money for it or else you're just solving a fake problem." — Varun Vummadi
